Herein, the reduced graphene oxide-wrapped open bimetallic sulfide (NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO) nanocage, derived from nickel–cobalt Prussian blue, was obtained by two-step calcination. There are luxuriant pore structures in the nanocage composite with a specific surface area of 85.28 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, which provides plentiful paths for rapid transmission of Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> and alleviates the volume stress caused by insertion and extraction of alkali metal ions. The excellent interface combination of bimetallic sulfide wrapped in reduced graphene oxide improves the conductivity and overall performance of the battery. Thanks to the special interface engineering, the open NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO nanocage composite displays rapid lithium storage properties with an average diffusion coefficient of 8.5 × 10<sup>–13</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>. Moreover, after 300 cycles, the reversible capacity of the composite is 1113.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. In SIBs, the capacity of the open NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO composite is 487.9 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> when the current density is 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>. These preeminent performances demonstrate the enormous development prospects of bimetallic sulfide nanocage as anode material in LIBs and SIBs.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D45619%26pageCount%3D13%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DYu-Jie%2BRen%252C%2BHao-Bo%2BGuan%252C%2BYun-Lei%2BHou%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D16%26issueNum%3D34%26contentID%3Dacsami.4c06039%26title%3DEnhancing%2BRapid%2BLi%252B%252FNa%252B%2BStorage%2BPerformance%2Bvia%2BInterface%2BEngineering%2Bof%2BReduced%2BGraphene%2BOxide-Wrapped%2BBimetallic%2BSulfide%2BNanocages%26numPages%3D13%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D45631%26publicationDate%3DAugust%2B2024" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2024 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries"> Batteries </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291331&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Composites"> Composites </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290920&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Nanoparticles"> Nanoparticles </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291840&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Sulfides"> Sulfides </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Bimetallic+Sulfide&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_bimetallic%20sulfide">bimetallic sulfide</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Reduced+Graphene+Oxide&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_reduced%20graphene%20oxide">reduced graphene oxide</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anode+Material&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anode%20material">anode material</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Open+Nanocage+Structure&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_open%20nanocage%20structure">open nanocage structure</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Rapid+Storage&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_rapid%20storage">rapid storage</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">With the rapid development of the economy, the energy problem is becoming more and more serious. And battery technology is an effective way to solve this problem, including lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), zinc-ion batteries (ZIBs), and so on. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1,2)</a> LIBs are emerging green battery technology with the advantages of high energy density, no memory effect, low self-discharge rate, and long cycle life. It dominates the development of electrochemical energy storage technology and has been applied to various fields such as portable electronic products, electric vehicles, and aerospace. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref4 ref5" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3 ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 3 to 5">(3−5)</a> At the same time, SIBs are a widely concerned issue because of their rich resources and low cost. It is worth noting that SIBs have a similar reaction mechanism to LIBs and can directly inherit the existing process equipment. Nevertheless, the radius of sodium ions is larger, which makes the sodiation and desodiation processes difficult. And the electrode structure is vulnerable to damage, leading to rapid attenuation of the capacity eventually. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 6 and 7">(6,7)</a> Therefore, it is extremely important to explore an electrode material with the merits of low price, good stability, and high specific capacity.</div><div class="NLM_p">Metal–organic framework (MOF) is a novel material with a controllable structure and large specific surface area, which is self-assembled by transition-metal ions and organic ligands. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 8 and 9">(8,9)</a> The porous structure effectively relieves the stress caused by volume change during the insertion and extraction of Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup>, and then improves the stability of the battery. Prussian blue (PB) and its analogues (PBA) are widely used as an important MOF material, which has the advantages of high capacity, low cost, and simple synthesis. However, PB/PBA obtained by the coprecipitation method has low conductivity and poor electrochemical performance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11 ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10 ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 10 to 12">(10−12)</a> Therefore, it is necessary to adopt some strategies to activate the storage sites of alkali metal ions. PB/PBA etching significantly improves battery performance. In the process of etching, the vertex of the original cube structure is preferentially etched to form a cage hollow structure. This specific structure exhibits a large specific surface area and enough ion channels, facilitating reversible insertion and disinsertion of Li<sup>+</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup>. Wang et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> designed NiCo(OH)<sub>2</sub>/NiS<sub>2</sub> heterogeneous cages with Ni–Co PBA using the anion etching method, which shows favorable activity and stability. In recent years, various MOF materials are used as precursors and treated by physical or chemical methods to obtain MOF derivatives, such as oxides, sulfides, and selenides.</div><div class="NLM_p">Transition-metal sulfide (TMS) is considered to be an outstanding electrode material because of good reversibility, high capacity, and low cost. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 14 and 15">(14,15)</a> Compared with oxide, transition-metal sulfide has a lower reaction energy barrier and better electrochemical activity due to the fact that the metal–S bond in metal sulfides is weaker than the metal–O bond in oxides. Thus, Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> has better kinetic properties in intercalation and deintercalation. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> It can be found that bimetallic sulfides have a special heterostructure and vast active sites. There is a synergistic effect between the two metal cations, which enhances the charge transfer velocity and improves the charge/discharge properties significantly. Rao et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> prepared hollow WS<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>@carbon microspheres/rGO composite, which demonstrates extraordinary sodium storage properties (411.8 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>). Unfortunately, TMS has inherent problems containing large volume expansion, uncontrollable side reactions, and agglomerative particles, which affect the comprehensive properties of the battery.</div><div class="NLM_p">To overcome these shortcomings, the nanosizing material is a common method. Nanostructure, such as nanowire, nanosphere, and nanocage, not only alleviates the volume stress generated by TMS conversion reaction but also shortens the transport distance and promotes ion migration. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 18 and 19">(18,19)</a> Another way is combining TSM with carbon nanotubes (CNTs), graphene, or MXene to form a conductive network structure. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 20 and 21">(20,21)</a> It further boosts the conductivity of the material, enhances the stability of the electrode interface, and alleviates the volume stress caused by the expansion effect. The above two methods show a synergistic effect, and the designed composites have excellent performance as an anode material. Zhang et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> synthesized Mn–Sn bimetallic sulfide combined with N-doped carbon nanorod, which was used in LIBs and has a high specific capacity. He et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> encapsulated SnS/Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> in sulfur–nitrogen codoped carbon material (SNC), and the product was obtained and named SnS/Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@SNC. The interface heterogeneous structure of bimetallic sulfide is beneficial for promoting the kinetics process of the reaction and SnS/Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@SNC displays satisfactory reversible capacity and rate performance. Deng et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> prepared NiS<sub>2</sub>@CoS<sub>2</sub> nanocube composite by chemical etching, which cycles over 800 times at a current density of 5 A g<sup>–1</sup> and maintains a stable capacity in SIBs. Therefore, the composites of MOF-derived bimetallic sulfides with carbon-based materials have a broad prospect in the energy storage field.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In this study, nickel cobalt Prussian blue analogue precursor (Ni–Co PBA) is synthesized via the coprecipitation method, and the reduced graphene oxide (GO)-wrapped open bimetallic sulfide (NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO) nanocage is successfully prepared by two-step calcination. The open nanocage structure and the coating of rGO provide numerous channels for ion and electron transport and accelerate the reaction kinetics. In addition, the excellent interface interactions of bimetallic sulfide wrapped in rGO improve the electrical conductivity, relieve volume stress, and reduce the agglomeration of the particles. As expected, open NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO composite exhibits remarkable cycle and rate performance when used as anode material.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Materials and Reagents</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Dopamine hydrochloride (C<sub>8</sub>H<sub>11</sub>NO<sub>2</sub>·HCl) and trihydroxymethyl aminomethane (Tris) were purchased from Aladdin, and their purities are 98% and 99.9%, respectively. Potassium cobalt cyanide (C<sub>6</sub>CoK<sub>3</sub>N<sub>6</sub>), also bought from Aladdin, is analytically pure level. Cetyltrimethylammonium bromide (CTAB) and nickel acetate (Ni(CH<sub>3</sub>COO)<sub>2</sub>) were bought from Fuchen Chemical Reagents Co., Ltd. Sulfur powder (purity: 99.5%) was also purchased from Aladdin. The concentrated ammonia–water (NH<sub>3</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O) was purchased from Nanning Guande Chemical Co., Ltd., with a purity of 99.9%. Analytical pure-grade anhydrous ethanol (C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>6</sub>O) and sodium citrate (C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>5</sub>Na<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O) were purchased from Beijing Chemical Plant.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Preparation of Graphene Oxide</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Graphene oxide (GO) has been synthesized by the Hummers method, and the detailed procedures were shown in our group’s previous work. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a></div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.3.  Synthesis of NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO</h3><div class="NLM_p">1.33 g of C<sub>6</sub>CoK<sub>3</sub>N<sub>6</sub>, 1.5 g of Ni(CH<sub>3</sub>COO)<sub>2</sub>, and 2.2 g of C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>5</sub>Na<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O were dissolved in deionized water (DW), respectively. After mixing and stirring the solutions, the following steps were centrifugation, filtration, and drying at 70 °C to gain Ni–Co PBA precursor. Subsequently, the Ni–Co PBA was dissolved in anhydrous ethanol and ammonia–water was added to etch with the etching time of 10, 15, and 20 min. Then centrifugation and drying were performed to obtain caged Ni–Co PBA.</div><div class="NLM_p">Cage Ni–Co PBA and C<sub>8</sub>H<sub>11</sub>NO<sub>2</sub>·HCl were dissolved in tris (PH = 8.5) with a mass ratio of 1:1. The following steps were successively stirring, centrifuging, collecting precipitation, cleaning with DW, and drying to get Ni–Co PBA@PDA. After these operations, Ni–Co PBA@PDA, CTAB, and GO were dissolved in DW with a mass ratio of 4:1:3 and stirred at room temperature (RT) for 2 h. Then the filtered precipitate was cleaned and freeze-dried. Eventually, the sample was calcined at 700 °C in Ar atmosphere for 6 h, and the cage Ni–Co PBA@rGO was obtained.</div><div class="NLM_p">0.1 g of cage Ni–Co PBA@rGO and 0.2 g of sulfur powder were calcined with a reaction temperature of 500 °C and a calcination time of 2 h to obtain cage NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO. Moreover, to explore the effects of GO concentrations on the properties of the materials, the ratios of Ni–Co PBA@PDA and GO were 1:0.5, 1:0.75, and 1:1, respectively, which were denoted as NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO-1, NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO-2, and NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO-3.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The cube NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO is the comparison sample in this study. The cube NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO was prepared in the same steps as cage NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO, except that there is no need for etching with ammonia–water.</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7">2.4.  Material Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The crystal structure of the cage NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO composite was obtained by an X-ray diffractometer (XRD, Ultima IV). The diffraction angle was 3–90°, and the scanning rate was 10° min<sup>–1</sup>. Raman spectroscopy (inVia Reflex) was employed to explore the molecular structure. The micromorphology of the materials was characterized by scanning electron microscopy (SEM, Hitachi S-4700) combined with energy-dispersive spectrum (EDS). To further investigate their internal structure, transmission electron microscopy (TEM, Hitachi HT-7700) was conducted for observation. In addition, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, ESCALAB 250) was exploited to identify the element composition and chemical bond on the surface of the composite. N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption test was utilized to investigate the specific surface area and pore size distribution of the NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO composite on JW-BK400. To study the content of each component in the composite, a thermal gravimetric analyzer (TGA, Setsysevoiution) was used.</div></div><div id="sec2_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8">2.5.  Electrochemical Tests</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The working electrode was prepared as follows: cage NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO composite, sodium carboxymethyl cellulose, and acetylene black were mixed with a mass ratio of 7:2:1 and dissolved in DW to form a uniform slurry. After coating on the copper foil, it was dried at 60 °C for 12 h in a vacuum oven. The CR2032 coin battery was assembled in a glovebox filled with Ar. In LIBs, the anode is a lithium tablet, the separator is polypropylene (PP) films, and the electrolyte is 1 M LiPF<sub>6</sub> in EC: DEC = 1:1 vol % + 5% FEC. For SIBs, sodium foil anode and fiberglass separator were used, and the electrolyte is 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in the same solvent as LIBs. Cycle voltammetry (CV) tests were carried out on the Versa CS350H electrochemical workstation with a voltage window of 0–3 V and a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was conducted by the same device, and the frequency was set to 0.01–10<sup>6</sup> Hz. Moreover, LAND CT 2001A was employed for the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) tests and charge and discharge tests between 0.01 and 3 V.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The schematic diagram of the synthesis process of open NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO nanocage has been shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>. Ni–Co PBA synthesized by coprecipitation was used as a precursor, and it was first etched with ammonia. Whereafter, dopamine hydrochloride and graphene oxide were combined with the etching product and the calcination was conducted to gain Ni–Co PBA@rGO under Ar atmosphere at 700 °C. Lastly, Ni–Co PBA@rGO was mixed with sulfur powder and calcined again at 500 °C to obtain bimetallic sulfide and reduced graphene oxide composite (NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO). Thanks to the open nanocage and the combination of NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> and rGO, it is expected to form a stable electrode surface and solid electrolyte interface (SEI), which is beneficial to electrochemical properties.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c06039/asset/images/medium/am4c06039_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c06039/asset/images/large/am4c06039_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Schematic diagram of the synthesis process of the cage NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO composite.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c06039/asset/images/large/am4c06039_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c06039&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">SEM was used to observe the microstructure of the caged NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO intermediate. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a displays the image of the Ni–Co PBA precursor with a cube structure. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c06039/suppl_file/am4c06039_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a> presents the etching products by ammonia with etching times of 0, 10, 15, and 20 min, respectively. The longer the etching time is, the more significant the morphology change is. The eight vertices of the Ni–Co PBA cube disappear preferentially and the planes hardly change, which can be attributed to the fact that the adsorption energy of vertices to ammonia–water is higher than that of edges and planes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> Ni–Co PBA etched for 15 min has a more obvious etching effect than that etched for 10 min, and all of the vertices of the cube Ni–Co PBA particle are lost. To further detect the interior structure, the product that was etched for 15 min was observed by TEM, which has been shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c. The etching process proceeds along the body diagonal of the cube and forms open nanocages ultimately. However, the morphology of the precursor undergoes significant change and collapses into a sheet structure completely with an etching time of 20 min. Therefore, Ni–Co PBA with an etching time of 15 min is selected to synthesize the final product. Compared with cube Ni–Co PBA, the cage Ni–Co PBA after etching has a hollow structure, which can facilitate the release of volume stress. Meantime, it also provides more active sites, shortens the charge transport paths, and thus achieves better reaction kinetics. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d shows the image of the cage Ni–Co PBA@PDA, and the dopamine hydrochloride particles are distributed around the Ni–Co–PBA nanocages uniformly.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c06039/asset/images/medium/am4c06039_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c06039/asset/images/large/am4c06039_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. SEM image of (a) Ni–Co PBA precursor; (b) SEM image and (c) TEM image of cage Ni–Co PBA with an etching time of 15 min; and (d) SEM image of cage Ni–Co PBA@PDA.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c06039/asset/images/large/am4c06039_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c06039&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>, SEM images of the cube NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO composite and the cage NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO composite are displayed. The microstructure of cube NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO has not changed significantly after sulfuration and calcination treatment (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a,<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b). NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@PBA nanoparticles with open nanocage structure are well encapsulated by rGO as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c,<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d. It can be found that the nanoparticle dispersion is even and the structure is integral. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c06039/suppl_file/am4c06039_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S2 and S3</a> present the EDS spectra images of cage NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO composite, which demonstrate the uniform distribution of the elements including Ni, Co, S, and C in the composite and prove the successful preparation of NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO nanocage composite. Therein, the atomic ratio of Ni and Co is about 1.35.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c06039/asset/images/medium/am4c06039_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c06039/asset/images/large/am4c06039_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. SEM images of (a, b) the cube NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO composite and (c, d) the cage NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO composite.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c06039/asset/images/large/am4c06039_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c06039&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a, the crystal phase composition of cage NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO is investigated by XRD. There are four obvious diffraction peaks located at 2θ = 27.2, 33.4, 38.78, and 53.64°, corresponding to the crystal planes of (111), (200), (211), and (311), respectively. The three peaks are well-matched to the characteristic peaks in the standard card of NiS<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS #11-0099), which proves the existence of NiS<sub>2</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 26 and 27">(26,27)</a> Moreover, three sharp peaks at 2θ = 26.9, 31.9, and 55.09° can be associated with (220), (311), and (440) lattice planes of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> (JCPDS #47-1738), indicating the presence of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> in the final composite. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> The successful synthesis of NiS<sub>2</sub> and Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles is testified by comparison with the characteristic peaks of the two substances in standard cards. In addition, the diffraction peaks of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> and NiS<sub>2</sub> in the XRD spectra are both strong and distinct without hybrid peaks, illustrating that the prepared cage NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO composite has high crystallinity and purity. Notably, the (002) plane of rGO around 2θ = 25° is not distinct, which is due to the fact that the peak positions between the (220) crystal plane of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>, (111) plane of NiS<sub>2</sub>, and (002) plane of rGO are close and overlap. The Raman spectrum of the composite is exhibited in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b. It can be noticed that there are two obvious peaks located at 1353 and 1583 cm<sup>–1</sup>. The peak at 1350 cm<sup>–1</sup> belongs to the D band and the one at 1583 cm<sup>–1</sup> is the G band. Generally, the area ratio of the two peaks is utilized to evaluate the defect degree of materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8 ref29" aria-label="reference 8 and 29">(8,29)</a> For cage NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO composite, the peak areas ratio of D band and G band is 2.24, indicating that the composite has abundant defects, which is conducive to Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> transmission. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a></div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c06039/asset/images/medium/am4c06039_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c06039/asset/images/large/am4c06039_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) XRD pattern; (b) Raman spectra; (c) XPS full spectrum; high-resolution XPS spectra for (d) S 2p; (e) Ni 2p; (f) Co 2p; (g) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption curve; (h) pore size distribution diagram; and (i) TGA curve of cage NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO composite.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c06039/asset/images/large/am4c06039_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c06039&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To further explore the chemical composition and element valence state of the composite surface, XPS was carried out and shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c–<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>f. In the full spectrum of the cage NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO composite (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c), elements C, S, Co, and Ni can be easily found, which is consistent with the mapping results. Moreover, the characteristic peaks of elements N and O are also present in the full spectrum. Nitrogen element may come from dopamine, and the oxygen element is derived from air and rGO. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d is the high-resolution spectrogram of S 2p and its subpeak fitting. It can be seen that three distinct peaks are located at 165.2, 163.9, and 161.7 eV, which belong to the S–C bond, S 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, and S 2p<sub>3/2</sub>, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9 ref31" aria-label="reference 9 and 31">(9,31)</a> The spin-orbit splitting peak of Ni is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>e. At binding energies of 870.8 and 853.2 eV, there are two splitting peaks corresponding to Ni<sup>2+</sup> 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Ni<sup>2+</sup> 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, respectively. The result illustrates the presence of NiS<sub>2</sub>. There are two other peaks appearing, and the peak values are 873.6 and 855.5 eV, which match Ni<sup>3+</sup> 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and Ni<sup>3+</sup> 2p<sub>3/2</sub>. And the reason for the existence of Ni<sup>3+</sup> can be attributed to the exposure of nanocage composite to air and partial Ni<sup>2+</sup> are oxidized to Ni<sup>3+</sup>. In addition, the satellite peaks of Ni<sup>2+</sup> 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Ni<sup>2+</sup> 2p<sub>1/2</sub> are at 863.1 and 882.1 eV. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 32 and 33">(32,33)</a> <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>f shows the XPS diagram of Co 2p. The subpeaks at 781.6, 784.9, 797.8, and 800.8 eV are associated with Co<sup>3+</sup> 2p<sub>3/2</sub>, Co<sup>2+</sup> 2p<sub>3/2</sub>, Co<sup>3+</sup> 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, and Co<sup>2+</sup> 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34 ref35 ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34 ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 34 to 36">(34−36)</a> In conclusion, XPS demonstrates that the cage NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO composites are prepared successfully, which illustrates the same results as XRD described above.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>g,<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>h shows the N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isothermal curve and pore size distribution of open NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO composite, respectively. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>g shows a typical class IV adsorption isotherm, indicating that there are abundant pores in the synthesized composite nanocages. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> The specific surface area of NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO is calculated by the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) equation, and the value is 85.28 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>. The larger specific surface area means that there are more active sites and contact areas, which is conducive to the rapid adsorption and deadsorption of Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup>, thereby improving the storage capacity and energy density of the battery. Besides, the large specific surface area is also beneficial for full contact of the electrode and electrolyte, forming a uniform solid electrolyte interface (SEI). SEI reduces the side reaction during the charge and discharge process and then extends the cycle life. In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>h, the pore diameter of the composite material is obtained by the Barrett–Joyner–Halenda (BJH) model. The distribution of the pore diameter is 15–40 nm, which demonstrates that the open NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO nanocage is a mesoporous material again. The presence of a large number of mesoporous provides more ion transport channels and releases the stress of volume expansion. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>i shows the thermogravimetric curve of the composite, which is employed to investigate the composition of cage NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO. The test was carried out with a temperature range from 40 to 800 °C under an air atmosphere. After heating, the mass residue is 28.33%, corresponding to NiO and CoO. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> The mass loss at 40–330 °C is mainly owing to the volatilization of surface-adsorbed H<sub>2</sub>O and the decomposition of surface unstable components, such as oxygen-containing functional groups on the surface of rGO. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> The mass decrease in the range of 330–570 °C can be attributed to two aspects: One is the transition from NiS<sub>2</sub> to NiS, accompanied by the release of SO<sub>2</sub>, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq 1</a>. The other is due to the combustion of rGO in the cage NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO composite. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40 ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 40 and 41">(40,41)</a> The last mass drop stage is associated with the conversion from transition-metal sulfide to oxide at 648–780 °C. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eqs 2">eqs 2</a> and <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="3">3</a>, NiS is converted to NiO, and Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> translates directly into more Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mi>NiS</mi><mn>2</mn></msub><mo>+</mo><msub><mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi><mn>2</mn></msub></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">→</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>NiS</mi><mo>+</mo><msub><mi>SO</mi><mn>2</mn></msub></mstyle></mrow></math></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mn>2</mn><mi>NiS</mi><mo>+</mo><mn>3</mn><msub><mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi><mn>2</mn></msub></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">→</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mn>2</mn><mi>NiO</mi><mo>+</mo><mn>2</mn><msub><mi>SO</mi><mn>2</mn></msub></mstyle></mrow></math></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq3"><div class="equation"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mi>Co</mi><mn>3</mn></msub><msub><mi mathvariant="normal">S</mi><mn>4</mn></msub><mo>+</mo><mn>6</mn><msub><mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi><mn>2</mn></msub></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">→</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mi>Co</mi><mn>3</mn></msub><msub><mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi><mn>4</mn></msub><mo>+</mo><mn>4</mn><msub><mi>SO</mi><mn>2</mn></msub></mstyle></mrow></math></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(3)</span></div></div></div><div class="NLM_p">To study the lithium storage mechanism of NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO, cycle voltammetry (CV) tests are performed with a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> and a voltage window of 0–3 V. The CV profile of cage NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO is displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a. It can be seen that there are two peaks located at 1.54 and 1.29 V, respectively, in the first cathode scan. In both reduction reactions, it binds to element S in the transition-metal sulfide to form Li<sub>2</sub>S along with the Li<sup>+</sup> insertion. The difference is the peak at 1.29 V corresponds to the fact that NiS<sub>2</sub> gains electrons and changes into the zerovalent Ni, while the other reduction peak appears on account of the reduction process of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> at 1.54 V. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> The specific reaction equations are shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq4" aria-label="eqs 4">eqs 4</a> and <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq5" aria-label="5">5</a>. The first reverse scanning process also has two peaks, corresponding to the removal of lithium ions and the oxidation of nickel and cobalt to Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> (2.07 V) and NiS<sub>2</sub> (2.51 V). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> Those are the reverse reactions of <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq4" aria-label="eqs 4">eqs 4</a> and <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq5" aria-label="5">5</a>. The CV profile of the second circle displays two reduction peaks of the cage NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO composite, which shift obviously. The reduction peaks of NiS<sub>2</sub> and Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> are transferred to 1.25 and 1.71 V, respectively. But the oxidation peaks did not shift significantly. This is due to the fact that the first cathodic scan process generates an irreversible solid electrolyte interface film in addition to the reduction reaction of transition-metal sulfide. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> Compared with the second cycle, the redox peaks of the third cycle barely changed. These results testify that the lithium storage behavior of the composite material includes two processes, and the nanocage composite has outstanding electrochemical reversibility. The CV curve of cube NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO composite is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>d, which is consistent with the trend of cage composite electrode, indicating that ammonia etching only changes the microstructure of the material, but does not affect the electrochemical behavior. At the same time, the last two cycles of the cube NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO composite also have a good overlap degree, which indicates that this electrode also has good reversibility.<div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq4"><div class="equation"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mi>NiS</mi><mn>2</mn></msub><mo>+</mo><mn>4</mn><msup><mi>Li</mi><mo>+</mo></msup><mo>+</mo><mn>4</mn><msup><mi mathvariant="normal">e</mi><mo>−</mo></msup></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">→</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>Ni</mi><mo>+</mo><mn>2</mn><msub><mi>Li</mi><mn>2</mn></msub><mi mathvariant="normal">S</mi></mstyle></mrow></math></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(4)</span></div></div><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq5"><div class="equation"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mrow><msub><mi>Co</mi><mn>3</mn></msub><msub><mi mathvariant="normal">S</mi><mn>4</mn></msub></mrow><mo>+</mo><mn>8</mn><msup><mi>Li</mi><mo>+</mo></msup><mo>+</mo><mn>8</mn><msup><mi mathvariant="normal">e</mi><mo>−</mo></msup></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">→</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mn>3</mn><mi>Co</mi><mo>+</mo><mn>4</mn><msub><mi>Li</mi><mn>2</mn></msub><mi mathvariant="normal">S</mi></mstyle></mrow></math></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(5)</span></div></div></div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c06039/asset/images/medium/am4c06039_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c06039/asset/images/large/am4c06039_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) CV curve and (b) charge and discharge curve of cage NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO; (c) GITT curve and Li<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients of the charge state of the two composites; (d) CV curve and (e) charge and discharge curve of cube NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO; (f) GITT curve and Li<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients of discharge state; (g) cycle performance (0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>); (h) rate performance; (i) a schematic diagram of lithium storage mechanism; (j) long cycle performance (1 A g<sup>–1</sup>); and (k) EIS and equivalent circuit diagram of cube and cage NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO composites in LIBs.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c06039/asset/images/large/am4c06039_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c06039&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>b,<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>e, voltage platforms during the charge and discharge processes are well-matched with peak potentials of redox reactions of CV tests. And the curves present a high coincidence degree during the cycles, which illustrates the admirable reversibility. The specific capacity of the open NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO nanocage is higher after 100 cycles, and its charge and discharge curves show better cyclic stability than those of the cube structure composite. During the charge/discharge process, open NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO nanocages can adapt to volume expansion, prevent electrode cracking, and slow down the decay of reversible capacity, which is significantly better than those of the cube composite. Except for reducing the adverse influences caused by volume stress, the high specific surface area of the cage structure composite can also provide more attachment sites for Li<sup>+</sup>, and then improve its storage property. Moreover, the unique microstructure of the cage NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO composite can shorten the diffusion path of Li<sup>+</sup> to a great extent and promote the migration of Li<sup>+</sup>.</div><div class="NLM_p">To explore the diffusion behavior of Li<sup>+</sup>, GITT tests were carried out with a voltage range of 0–3 V at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, and the results are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c,<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>f. According to <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq6" aria-label="eq 6">eq 6</a>, Li<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients (<i>D</i><sub>Li+</sub>) of the two composites with different structures are calculated, respectively, when the batteries are charging and discharging. It can be clearly seen that the <i>D</i><sub>Li+</sub> of the nanocage NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO electrode (8.5 × 10<sup>–13</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>) is larger than the cube NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO electrode, which manifests that the open nanocage composite obtained by etching is more conducive to the transfer and diffusion process of electrons and ions. The enhanced performance can be attributed to two points: First, NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO nanocage has a more mesoporous structure, which provides a large number of channels for fast transmission of Li<sup>+</sup>. Posteriorly, the work function of NiS<sub>2</sub> is 4.62 eV, while that of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> is 4.36 eV. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44 ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44 ref45" aria-label="reference 44 and 45">(44,45)</a> The differences in the work functions of the two transition-metal sulfides induce space charge and built-in electric field. These will drive the charge flow from higher to lower energy levels until a thermodynamic equilibrium is reached. Therefore, the interface engineering design of bimetallic sulfide heterojunction enhances the charge transfer and reduces the diffusion energy barrier, thereby boosting the kinetic behavior for redox reactions.<div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq6"><div class="equation"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>D</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mfrac><mn>4</mn><mrow><mi>π</mi><mi>τ</mi></mrow></mfrac><msup><mrow><mo stretchy="true">(</mo><mfrac><mrow><msub><mi>n</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></msub><msub><mi>V</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></msub></mrow><mi>S</mi></mfrac><mo stretchy="true">)</mo></mrow><mn>2</mn></msup><msup><mrow><mo stretchy="true">(</mo><mfrac><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi><msub><mi>E</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">s</mi></msub></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi><mi>E</mi><mi>τ</mi></mrow></mfrac><mo stretchy="true">)</mo></mrow><mn>2</mn></msup></mstyle></mrow></math></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(6)</span></div></div>where τ represents relaxation time and <i>S</i> stands for contact area. <i>V</i><sub>m</sub> and <i>n</i><sub>m</sub> are the molar volume and the amount of substance of the electrode material, respectively. Δ<i>E</i><sub>s</sub> and Δ<i>E</i><sub>τ</sub> are the voltage changes caused by the pulse and the constant current charge and discharge, respectively.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>g shows the cycle performance of cage and cube NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO composites used as anode materials. The initial charge and discharge capacities of the cage NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO composite are 2006.7 and 1372.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> when the current density is 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. In comparison, the cube composite only shows 1855.5 and 1280.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, which are significantly lower than those of the cage NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO. The composites exhibit ultrahigh-specific capacity during the cycle. Owing to the particular morphology and structure of rGO, lithium ions have nonstoichiometric insertion/extraction during the charge and discharge process, which leads to greater lithium storage capacity. Moreover, metal nanoparticles have strong surface capacitance, and numerous spin polarization electrons are deposited in the spin splitting band of the metal-reduced nanoparticles, contributing to an extra capacity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> The initial Coulomb efficiencies (ICE) of the two anodes are only 68.9% and 69.01%, which are related to the SEI formation in the first cycle. For the nanocage NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO, the discharge capacity (1392 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) and capacity retention rate (85.2%) are both higher than those of the nanocube composite (838 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and 63.2%) after 100 cycles. In conclusion, the nanocage NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO composite has excellent cyclic stability for the following reasons: On the one hand, an open nanocage structure offers a larger specific surface area and higher porosity. The large specific surface area means plenty of active sites, and the porous structure enhances the kinetic process of the reaction. In addition, the open nanocage composite effectively alleviates the volume stress generated by the intercalation and removal of Li<sup>+</sup>, which maintains the stability of the electrode structure.</div><div class="NLM_p">In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>h, the rate tests are conducted to investigate the rapid storage performance of lithium ions when the cube and cage NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO composites are used under a large current. The open NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO composite delivers the discharge capacities of 1212.7, 1089.3, 974.6, 897.3, 825.6, and 700.4 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, and 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. The capacities of the cube composite are much lower than that of the former at any current density. In particular, the specific capacity of cube material is 186.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, which is 514.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> lower than that of nanocage NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO. When reverting to a low current density (0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>), the reversible capacity of cage NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO can restore to 1231 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and displays an outstanding electrochemical property. As a contrast, the specific capacity of cube NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO decreases overtly, which is due to the microstructure of the material being damaged irreversibly at high current density. The long cycle performance is performed at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>j. After 250 cycles, the discharge capacities of the nanocage NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO and the nanocube NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO are 1152.5 and 641.4 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Thanks to the nanocage structure, the open NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO composite possesses a higher specific capacity. This means that there are more lithium storage sites on the surface of the material, the insertion/removal behavior is faster, and the electrolyte is more fully infiltrated in the battery. Therefore, the battery has a high specific capacity at a high current and long cycle, which is much better than similar previous works (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c06039/suppl_file/am4c06039_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>, Supporting Information). An interesting phenomenon is that the specific capacities of the two materials enlarge slightly with the increase of the cycle number, which can be owing to the following points: First, lithium ions can form a metal-rich lithium phase after intercalation reaction, which has a positive effect on capacity increase. In addition, the edge effect and surface effect of graphene interact, which is conducive to the continuous wetting of the electrolyte and improving the accessibility of lithium ions. And the change of the electrode structure increases the accumulation of lithium atoms, thus enhancing the specific capacity of the battery. Moreover, Stone–Wales defects increase during lithium-ion insertion and extraction, thereby helping to enhance the capacity of the electrode. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47 ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref47 ref48" aria-label="reference 47 and 48">(47,48)</a> As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>i, the open nanocage structure provides more transport channels, promotes lithium-ion migration, and improves the kinetic process of the redox reaction.</div><div class="NLM_p">The EIS tests of the two composites were used to further explore the electrochemical kinetics in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>k. It can be observed that the images of cage and cube composites are both composed of two parts: one is a semicircle reflecting the charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) in high-frequency region. The other is a straight line representing Warburg impedance in low-frequency region, which indicates that the electrode is affected by diffusion. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> The resistance of the two composites was fitted using the equivalent circuit in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>k, and the results are shown in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table 1</a>. The <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of the cage and cube NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO composites are 160.1 and 250.8 Ω, respectively. Moreover, it can be seen from <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq7" aria-label="eqs 7">eqs 7</a>–<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq9" aria-label="9">9</a> that the reciprocal of the impedance real part and the square root of the frequency have a linear relationship. And the smaller the slope of the fitted line, the better the ion diffusion performance. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c06039/suppl_file/am4c06039_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>, cage NiS<sub>2</sub>–Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@rGO has a smaller slope and faster ion diffusion. This demonstrates that the open nanocage structure can lower charge transfer impedance and promote the rapid transport of Li<sup>+</sup>, which is consistent with the GITT results.<div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq7"><div class="equation"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em"
